<li>Consider withdrawal symptoms and how to avoid or reduce them. Provide information and management of methadone toxicity.</li>
<li>Consider options for counselling and/or rehabilitation.</li>
</ul>
<p>For patients willing to stop drug use:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Set a definite day to quit.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Consider withdrawal symptoms and how to manage them.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Discuss strategies of avoiding or coping with high risk situations.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Make specific plans to avoid drug use, for example, how to respond to friends who still use drugs.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Identify family or friends who will support stopping drug use.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Consider options for counselling and/or rehabilitation.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>In the case of patients who do not succeed or relapse:</p>
<ul>
<li>Identify and give credit for any success.</li>
<li>Discuss situations that led to the relapse.</li>
<li>Return to earlier steps.</li>
</ul>
<p>The medication to be used varies according to the specific drug withdrawal. You should always make sure that you do not only give the patient the right medication, but that you are also able to help the patient deal with life problems, employment, social relationships, etc. This is a very important component of the treatment process.</p>
<p>Alcohol Abuse</p>
<p>Alcohol misuse is another common substance abuse problem. Patients may present with:</p>
<ul>
<li>Depressed mood.</li>
<li>Nervousness.</li>
<li>Insomnia.</li>
<li>Physical complications of alcohol use, for example, gastrointestinal ulceration, gastritis, liver disease, and hypertension.</li>
<li>Accidents or injuries due to alcohol use.</li>
<li>Poor memory or concentration.</li>
<li>Evidence of self-neglect.</li>
<li>Poor compliance to management for depression.</li>
</ul>
<p>The patient may also have experienced legal and social problems due to alcohol use, for example, marital problems, domestic violence, child abuse and neglect or missed work. The individual may show signs of alcohol withdrawal, which include sweating, tremors, morning sickness, hallucinations and seizures.</p>
<p>Patients may sometimes deny or are unaware of alcohol problems. Family members may request help before the patient does. The problem may also be identified during a routine health promotion screening.</p>
<p>Diagnostic Features of Alcohol Abuse</p>
<p>Harmful alcohol use refers to the consumption of over 28 units per week for men and over 21 units per week for women. This can result in physical harm, for example, liver disease,</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>gastrointestinal bleeding, psychological harm, for instance, depression or anxiety or social consequences, like the loss of a job.</p>
<p>Alcohol dependence is said to be present when any of the following factors are present:</p>
<ul>
<li>Strong desire or compulsion to use alcohol.</li>
<li>Difficulty controlling alcohol use.</li>
<li>Withdrawal symptoms, for example, anxiety, tremors, sweating, when drinking is ceased.</li>
</ul>
<p>Tolerance refers to the individual who is able to drink large amounts of alcohol without appearing intoxicated. They continue alcohol use despite the harmful consequences.</p>
<p>Blood tests such as Gamma-Glutamyltransferase (GGT) and Mean Corpuscular Volume (MCV) can help identify heavy alcohol drinkers.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="management-of-alcohol-abuse">Management of Alcohol Abuse<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management-of-alcohol-abuse" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>There are many similarities in the management strategies used for patients who have substance abuse problems and those who have alcohol abuse problems. For patients with physical illness and/or dependency or failed attempts at controlled drinking, an abstinence programme is indicated.</p>
<p>If you have a patient who is willing to stop now:</p>
<ul>
<li>Set a definite day to quit.</li>
<li>Consider withdrawal symptoms and how to manage them.</li>
<li>Discuss strategies of avoiding drinking, for example, ways to respond to friends who still drink.</li>
<li>Consider options for support after withdrawal.</li>
</ul>
<p>For patients not willing to stop or reduce alcohol use immediately, a harm reduction programme may be indicated:</p>
<ul>
<li>Do not reject or blame the patient.</li>
<li>Point out clearly medical, psychological and social problems caused by alcohol use.</li>
<li>Consider thiamine preparations (150mg per day in divided doses should be given for one month).</li>
<li>Make a future appointment to reassess health and alcohol use.</li>
</ul>
<p>In the case of patients who do not succeed or have a relapse, you should identify the problem that caused the relapse and give the patient credit for any success. You should also discuss</p>
<p>situations that led to the relapse and return to earlier steps.</p>
<p>You may wish to recommend self-help organisations, for example, Alcoholics Anonymous. Most voluntary and non-statutory agencies are often very helpful.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="medication-for-alcohol-abuse">Medication for Alcohol Abuse<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#medication-for-alcohol-abuse" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>For patients with mild withdrawal symptoms, frequent monitoring, support, reassurance, adequate hydration and nutrition are sufficient treatment without medication.</p>
<p>Patients with moderate withdrawal syndrome will also require benzodiazepines. Most can be detoxified as outpatients or at home. Only practitioners with appropriate training and supervision should do community detoxification. Inpatient detoxification is indicated for:</p>
<ul>
<li>Patients at risk of complicated withdrawal syndrome, for example, with a history of fits or delirium, tremors, very heavy use and high tolerance.</li>
<li>Significant polydrug use or severe morbid medical or psychiatric disorder who lack social support or are considered to have a significant suicide risk. In such cases, chlordiazepoxide (librium) at 10mg is recommended. The initial dose should be considered against withdrawal symptoms within a range of 5-40mg four times a day. This requires close and skilled supervision.</li>
</ul>
<p>The regimen opposite can be used although the dose level and length of treatment will depend on the severity of alcohol dependence and individual patient factors, for example, weight, sex and liver function. Treatment should be dispensed daily. You may want to involve a family member to prevent the risk of misuse or overdose.</p>
<p>Thiamine (150mg per day in divided doses) should be given orally for one month. Parenteral thiamine is indicated for patients with ataxia, confusion, memory disturbances, delirium tremens, hypothermia and hypotension, ophthalmoplegia or unconsciousness.</p>
<table><thead><tr><th></th><th></th><th></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Day 1 and 2</td><td>20-30mg</td><td>QDS</td></tr><tr><td>Day 3 and 4</td><td>15mg</td><td>QDS</td></tr><tr><td>Day 5</td><td>10mg</td><td>QDS</td></tr><tr><td>Day 6</td><td>10mg</td><td>QDS</td></tr><tr><td>Day 7</td><td>10mg</td><td>QDS</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Daily Treatment</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>An expert should be involved in case monitoring for the first few days.</p>
<p>When depression concurs with alcohol misuse, Selective Serotonin Re-uptake Inhibitors (SSRI) may be used. These include fluoxetine, paroxetine and citalopram. Tricyclic antidepressants should be avoided because of tricyclic-alcohol interactions. For anxiety, benzodiazepines should be avoided because of high potential for abuse.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="referral">Referral<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#referral" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The patient should be referred for hospital detoxification, if they do not meet the criteria for community detoxification.</p>
<p>In addition, the patient should also be referred for targeted counselling, if available, to deal with the social causes or consequences of drinking, for example, relationship counselling.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="organic-disorders">Organic Disorders<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#organic-disorders" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Organic disorders represent a group of mental disorders that present a variety of symptoms, especially a disturbance of cognition. Delirium is common in general hospitals with an estimated 5%-15% of patients exhibiting the symptoms. Meanwhile, dementia impairs about 1,000,000 people. Alzheimer's disease is the most common type of organic disorder.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="delirium">Delirium<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#delirium" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Families may request help because the patient is confused or agitated. The patients may appear uncooperative or fearful. Occasionally, delirium may occur in patients hospitalised for physical conditions.</p>
<p>Diagnostic features include an acute onset, usually over hours or days, of confusion where the patient is disoriented and struggles to understand surroundings. This is evidenced by clouded thinking or awareness. This condition is often accompanied by:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Poor memory.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Agitation.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Emotional upset.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Loss of orientation.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Wandering attention.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Auditory hallucinations such as hearing voices.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Visual hallucinations.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Withdrawal from others.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Illusions.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Disturbed sleep (reversal of sleep patterns).</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Autonomic features, for example, sweating, tachycardia.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Some of the main causes of this condition have been identified as:</p>
<ul>
<li>Alcohol intoxication or withdrawal.</li>
<li>Drug intoxication, overdose or withdrawal.</li>
<li>Infection.</li>
<li>Metabolic changes, for example, liver disease, dehydration, hypoglycaemia.</li>
<li>Head trauma.</li>
<li>Hypoxia.</li>
<li>Epilepsy.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="medication-for-delirium">Medication for Delirium<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#medication-for-delirium" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>When prescribing medication, you should avoid the use of sedatives or hypnotic medications, for example, benzodiazepines, except for the treatment of alcohol or sedative withdrawal. Antipsychotic medication in low doses may be needed to control agitation, psychotic symptoms or aggression.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="drugs-with-anticholinergic-action-and-antiparkinsonian-medication-can-exacerbate-or-cause-delirium">Drugs with anticholinergic action and antiparkinsonian medication can exacerbate or cause delirium.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#drugs-with-anticholinergic-action-and-antiparkinsonian-medication-can-exacerbate-or-cause-delirium" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>You should offer advice and support to the patient and their family. You should also prevent the patient from harming themselves or others, for example, by removing unsafe objects or restraining the patient if necessary. In addition, ensure that supportive contact with familiar people is maintained so as to reduce confusion. Keep in mind that hospitalisation may be required because of agitation or physical illness, which is causing the delirium.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="referral-for-delirium">Referral for Delirium<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#referral-for-delirium" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Referral to secondary mental health services is rarely indicated. However, referral to a physician is always necessary if the cause of the dementia is unclear or if the patient presents with drug, alcohol withdrawal, overdose or any other underlying condition that requires in-patient medical care.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="dementia">Dementia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#dementia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is a slow deterioration in cognitive functioning, causing multiple changes. This condition is common in individuals of advanced age, that is, 65 years and above and quite rare in youth or middle age.</p>
<p>Presenting complaints for this condition include:</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<ul>
<li>Patients may complain of forgetfulness, decline in mental functioning or they may feel depressed but may be unaware of the memory loss.</li>
<li>Families may ask for help initially because of failing memory, change in personality or behaviour and disorientation.</li>
<li>Changes in behaviour and functioning, for example, poor personal hygiene or social integration.</li>
<li>Decline in memory, thinking, judgment, orientation and language.</li>
<li>Being apathetic or disinterested.</li>
<li>Decline in every day functioning, for example, dressing, washing, cooking.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="senile-dementia">Senile Dementia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#senile-dementia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is a disorder of unknown cause characterised by severe impairment of intellectual functioning. If it occurs before the age of 65 years it is called Alzheimer's disease.</p>
<p>The treatment of dementia is directed towards the elimination of the physical cause, however, there is no specific treatment in the majority of cases. Treatment may include continuous assessment for cause of symptoms and treatment as indicated, group and/or individual psychotherapy with focus on the management of anxiety, loss, impairment, impending death, reality orientation and changes in lifestyle.</p>
<p>Non-pharmacological methods are often tried first in dealing with difficult behaviour. Antipsychotic medication in very low doses may be needed occasionally to manage aggression or restlessness. If possible avoid using sedatives or hypnotics, for example, benzodiazepines. Aspirin in low doses may be prescribed in vascular dementia in order to slow deterioration.</p>
<p>Health education includes:</p>
<ul>
<li>Reduction of stress and introduction of healthy habits in daily living.</li>
<li>Acceptance of a spouse as they deteriorate.</li>
<li>Involvement of the family to avoid anxiety-provoking situations.</li>
<li>Developing ways of combating disorientation, for example, appointment calendars, clocks, stable routines, set places for important items, etc.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="referral-for-senile-dementia">Referral for Senile Dementia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#referral-for-senile-dementia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The patient should be referred to a specialist to confirm diagnosis in complicated cases. They may also be referred to social services for practical help, for example, home help and day care. If there are behavioural problems, the patient should be referred to a psychiatrist. These usually involve complex family relationships and might be precursors to a depressive or psychotic episode</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="psychoses-associated-with-pregnancy-and-childbirth">Psychoses Associated with Pregnancy and Childbirth<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#psychoses-associated-with-pregnancy-and-childbirth" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>These are psychiatric illnesses that have been precipitated by either pregnancy or childbirth. They may occur during pregnancy or within six weeks after delivery. The peak is common within two weeks after delivery. In pregnancy, these disorders often occur during the first and third trimester.</p>
<p>Unwanted pregnancies are associated with anxiety and depression in the first trimester. In the third trimester, there may be fears related to the impending delivery or doubts about the normality of the foetus.</p>
<p>The condition is more frequent in:</p>
<ul>
<li>Primigravidas.</li>
<li>Those who have suffered previous major psychiatric illness.</li>
<li>Those with history of mental illness.</li>
<li>Those with serious medical problems affecting the course of the pregnancy, for example, diabetes mellitus. (Obstetricians and midwives consider mothers with these conditions as mothers at risk.)</li>
</ul>
<p>The causes of psychoses associated with pregnancy and childbirth are not conclusive. However, it has been proposed that there are several possible causal factors.</p>
<p>These include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Pre-existing predisposition. Some women have a hereditary or personality predisposition to develop mental illness. The women are asymptomatic until pregnancy or delivery which then acts as a stressor precipitating psychosis.</li>
<li>Hormonal changes, that is, the early onset of puerperal psychosis has led to speculations that they may be caused by hormonal changes.</li>
<li>Psychological factors, which refer to psychological stresses, especially</li>
</ul>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>during the first and third trimester when the anxiety level is high.</p>
<ul>
<li>Social factors, that is, pre-existing marital constraints, for example, lack of social support from the husband.</li>
<li>Biological factors usually associated with physical illness.</li>
</ul>
<p>There are several psychiatric conditions that are precipitated by factors associated with pregnancy and childbirth.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="acute-organic-psychosis">Acute Organic Psychosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#acute-organic-psychosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This has declined with good obstetric care but can occur when care is inadequate, for instance, puerperal sepsis.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="functional-disorders">Functional Disorders<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#functional-disorders" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>These can occur in the form of manic-depressive psychosis, depressive or manic phase. Depression is the most common form, and may result in suicidal ideation, guilt and/or negative feelings towards the baby. For example, the mother may think that the child has a serious ailment and for that reason she should kill it to save it from suffering.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="schizophrenia">Schizophrenia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#schizophrenia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This tends to develop sooner and is more acute than depression.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="schizoaffective-disorders">Schizoaffective Disorders<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#schizoaffective-disorders" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Schizoaffective disorders incorporate features of schizophrenia and affective disorder, which appear in the same person at the same time.</p>
<p>The clinical presentation will depend on the presenting condition.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="management">Management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Medical treatment will depend on the form the illness has taken. Patients who are moderately to severely depressed, usually benefit from ECT. This enables the patient to recover quickly so that they can resume the care of the baby. If the patient is less depressed, antidepressants may be tried first.</p>
<p>In schizophrenic patients, phenothiazines are given. If improvements are delayed, phenothiazines may be combined with ECT. In puerperal sepsis, appropriate antibiotics must be given.</p>
<p>Great care must be taken in the use of psychotropic drugs because of the risk of foetal malformations, impaired growth and pre-natal problems.</p>
<p>Where possible, avoid using tricyclic antidepressants or neuroleptics unless there are compelling clinical indications. Where the illness is endangering the patient's life and no alternative treatment is available or favourable, then termination of pregnancy should be considered.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="nursing-management">Nursing Management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nursing-management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Nursing management depends on the form the illness has taken. The patient should be nursed in a therapeutic environment. For example, a depressed patient may be contemplating suicide and should, therefore, be nursed on the ground floor to prevent her jumping to her death. Preventive precautions necessary for suicidal patients must be taken.</p>
<p>Where facilities are adequate, a separate nursery where the child can be nursed must be utilised.</p>
<p>In order to promote lactation, a well balanced diet rich in proteins and a lot of fluids must be provided.</p>
<p>As the patient improves, involve her in occupational therapy and psychotherapeutic group activities in order to rehabilitate and socialise her. You should also provide health education for the husband and other relatives on the causes, course and treatment of the illness.</p>
<p>The best way of preventing this condition is:</p>
<ul>
<li>Identification of risk groups.</li>
<li>Good obstetrics care during antenatal, delivery and postnatal periods.</li>
<li>Supportive psychotherapy.</li>
<li>Health education on clinic follow-up and drug compliance.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="abnormalities-of-sexual-preference-paraphilias">Abnormalities of Sexual Preference (Paraphilias)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#abnormalities-of-sexual-preference-paraphilias" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This concept has three dimensions. The first dimension is related to social life. The behaviour of the individual does not conform to what is generally regarded as normal behaviour. However, you should note that the accepted view of what is normal in one community does not necessarily apply to other communities. The second dimension is concerned with the harm that may be inflicted on the sex partner. The third dimension concerns itself with the suffering experienced by the persons themselves.</p>
<p>There are various ways through which sexual preference abnormalities may reach a medical practitioner. One way is through direct approach by the persons themselves. Another situation is where the patient will seek sexual help for the</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>spouse or other sexual partner. Sometimes the patients may present themselves as having sexual dysfunction and it is only with time that the doctor will discover the abnormality of sexual preference.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="abnormalities-of-sexual-preference-of-sexual-object">Abnormalities of Sexual Preference of Sexual Object<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#abnormalities-of-sexual-preference-of-sexual-object" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>These abnormalities are usually divided into two groups. The first one is the abnormalities of the object of the person's sexual drives. The second one refers to the abnormalities in the preference of the sexual act. You will now take a look at a few abnormalities of sex object. This mainly involves preference for an object other than another adult in the achievement of sexual excitement.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="paedophilia">Paedophilia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#paedophilia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Sexual excitement is achieved through having sexual activity or through fantasy of such activity with pre-pubertal children.</p>
<p>Treatment involves behavioural therapy and group treatment, which have, unfortunately, had little success. General measures (to be found at the end of this section) can also be tried.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="fetishism">Fetishism<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#fetishism" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This involves the use of an inanimate object as a means of attaining sexual excitement. It could also involve parts of the human body that are not related with sex act. It is not uncommon for men to be sexually excited by a part or clothing of a female. However, the behaviour becomes abnormal when it takes the precedence over usual sexual intercourse.</p>
<p>Psychoanalysis and behaviour therapy are the recommended forms of treatment. The general measures at the end of the section can be applied as well.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="abnormalities-in-preference-of-sexual-act">Abnormalities in Preference of Sexual Act<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#abnormalities-in-preference-of-sexual-act" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This group of abnormalities involves a variation in the behaviour that is carried out so as to get sexual arousal. The behaviour is usually directed towards adults but occasionally children are involved. Exhibitionism is an example of such behaviour.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="exhibitionism">Exhibitionism<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#exhibitionism" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is the repeated exposure of genitals to unprepared strangers in order to get sexual excitement but with no further effort to have sex with the stranger concerned. The average age is 20-40 years and the condition is usually found in men (Gayford, cited by Gelder et al., 2000).</p>
<p>The treatment approach that has been found to be most effective is a combination of counselling and behavioural techniques. Counselling deals with personal relationships while behaviour deals with self-monitoring. Self-monitoring involves the identification of circumstances that initiated the behaviour so as to avoid them.</p>
<p>Drugs that reduce sexual drive like cyproterone acetate have also been used, however, they are not generally recommended due to uncertain results and problems.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="abnormalities-of-gender-identity">Abnormalities of Gender Identity<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#abnormalities-of-gender-identity" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Transsexuals have been grouped under this category. People who are transsexual have a strong belief that they are of a gender opposite to that signified by their external genitalia. The person concerned has a strong urge to live like a member of the opposite sex. They feel strange in their bodies and want to change their body appearance to suit the desired gender.</p>
<p>Although transsexualism is a psychological problem, people often seek treatment that is aimed at the body and not the mind. Physical changes are not recommended. However, if a decision is made to undertake them, it should be made in stages, despite the radical demand by the patient, so as to give the patient time to adjust to the changes they are undergoing, as well as to evaluate the decisions that have been made.</p>
<p>The treatment that is most recommended is supportive psychotherapy. The therapist tries to convince the patient to accept the current status. Unfortunately, this is rarely successful.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="general-assessment-and-management-of-abnormalities-of-sexual-preference">General Assessment and Management of Abnormalities of Sexual Preference<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#general-assessment-and-management-of-abnormalities-of-sexual-preference" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The first step in the assessment is to exclude mental illness since abnormal sexual preference, is commonly secondary to dementia, alcoholism, depressive disorders or mania. These illnesses are thought to release previous fantasies that had not been acted upon. This is mainly true if the onset is middle age or later.</p>
<p>An inquiry should be made into sexual practices bearing in mind that one can have diverse sexual practices. Details of normal heterosexual vigour and interest are taken both in the present and in the past. If possible, an interview should be arranged with the patient's regular partner. The patient should be asked to explain what role the abnormal sexual preference is playing in their life. For example, it could be providing</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>comfort in addition to being a source of sexual excitement.</p>
<p>Patients seek treatment for paraphilias due to different motives. Some do it because their sexual partner, relative or police know about it. Though treatment is sought, the patient may have little desire to change since they are happy when told that nothing can be done. Occasionally, patients may seek treatment due to depression caused by guilt. During such moments, the urge to change is high but this often fades when normal mood is restored. The recommended form of treatment is psychotherapy, behaviour therapy and psychoanalysis.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="neurotic-disorders-in-adolescents-and-adults">Neurotic Disorders in Adolescents and Adults<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#neurotic-disorders-in-adolescents-and-adults" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Anxiety disorders are a group of mental disorders in which anxiety is the main concern. The psychodynamic theory views anxiety as arising from conflicts that are usually sexual and aggressive. There are several types of anxiety:</p>
<ul>
<li>Superego anxiety, for example, fear of being found guilty.</li>
<li>Castration anxiety, for example, fear of bodily mutilation.</li>
<li>Separation anxiety, for example, fear of loss of significant relationship.</li>
<li>Impulsive anxiety, for example, fear of the control of impulses.</li>
<li>Obsessive-compulsive disorders arise during the developmental stage. The patient uses the defence mechanism of undoing, isolation and reaction formation.</li>
<li>Phobic disorder is characterised by the use of the defence mechanism of displacement and avoidance to deal with castration anxiety and oedipal drives.</li>
<li>Automatic learning of responses to situations that are anxiety provoking.</li>
</ul>
<p>You will now move on to look at the clinical manifestation and management of selected types of anxiety disorders.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="generalised-anxiety-disorders">Generalised Anxiety Disorders<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#generalised-anxiety-disorders" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Generalised anxiety disorders usually present with tension-related physical symptoms, for example, headache, pounding heart, insomnia, and/or sweating.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="diagnostic-features-of-anxiety-disorders">Diagnostic Features of Anxiety Disorders<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#diagnostic-features-of-anxiety-disorders" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>There are multiple symptoms of anxiety or tension, which include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Physical arousal, for example, dizziness, sweating, a fast pounding heart, a dry mouth, stomach pains or chest pains.</li>
<li>Mental tension, for example, weariness, feeling tense or nervous, poor concentration, fear that something dangerous will happen and not being able to cope.</li>
<li>Physical tension, for example, restlessness, headache, tremors or an inability to relax.</li>
</ul>
<p>Symptoms may last for months and recur regularly. Often they are triggered by stressful events in those prone to worry.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="management-of-anxiety-disorders">Management of Anxiety Disorders<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management-of-anxiety-disorders" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Medication is secondary to treatment in the management of general anxiety. If anxiety symptoms exist, anti-anxiety medication, for example, diazepam may be used. However, it should not be used for longer than two weeks so as to avoid dependence.</p>
<p>Antidepressants, for instance, imipramine or clomipramine may also be used, especially if symptoms of depression are present. These drugs do not cause dependence but can lead to withdrawal symptoms, therefore, they should be tapered gradually.</p>
<p>Other forms of management include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Psychotherapy, used in various forms, with focus on analysing unconscious conflicts, interpersonal conflicts, or providing supportive measures.</li>
<li>Relaxation technique which helps the patient not to be tense and to relax.</li>
<li>Teaching the patient ways of monitoring themselves for increasing anxiety and taking appropriate action. This is termed as self-care and is especially applicable in cases of milder anxiety.</li>
</ul>
<p>Hospitalisation is used for short periods when symptoms become intense and family ability to give support at that time is limited.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="conversive-and-dissociative-disorders">Conversive and Dissociative Disorders<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#conversive-and-dissociative-disorders" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Patients present with unusual or dramatic physical symptoms, such as severe amnesia, trance, loss of sensation, visual disturbances, paralysis, aphasia, identity loss, confusion or possession states. The patients are not</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>malingering since they are not aware of their role in their symptoms.</p>
<p>Diagnostic features include physical symptoms that are unusual in presentation and not consistent with known disease. The onset may be sudden and related to psychological stress or difficult personal circumstances.</p>
<p>In acute cases, symptoms may be dramatic and unusual, may change from time to time and may be related to attention from others.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="differential-diagnosis">Differential Diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#differential-diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>You should ensure that you have carefully considered physical conditions that may cause the same symptoms and rule them out through full history, physical examination and laboratory investigations.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="management">Management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>When medicating the patient, you should avoid anxiolytics or sedatives. In more chronic cases with depressive symptoms, antidepressants may be helpful.</p>
<p>The patient and the family should be informed that physical or neurological symptoms often have no clear cause. Symptoms can be brought about by stress. They should also be aware that symptoms usually resolve rapidly, from hours to a few weeks, leaving no permanent damage.</p>
<p>The following advice should be given to both the patient and their family:</p>
<ul>
<li>Encourage the patient to acknowledge recent stresses or difficulties.</li>
<li>Advise the patient to take a brief rest and relief from stress, then return to usual activities.</li>
<li>Advise against prolonged rest or withdrawal from activities.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="phobic-and-anxiety-disorders">Phobic and Anxiety Disorders<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#phobic-and-anxiety-disorders" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>These are disorders having generalised anxiety only in particular circumstances - these people are free from anxiety most of the time. Another characteristic of phobic disorders is avoidance of the</p>
<p>feared object.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="simple-phobia">Simple Phobia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#simple-phobia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is a disorder characterised by inappropriate anxiety in the presence of the feared object or situation. For example:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Arachnophobia, that is, fear of spiders.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Acrophobia, that is, fear of heights.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Others include phobia of dental treatment, phobia of flying, and phobia of illness.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Simple phobias usually start in childhood and persist until adulthood. Under stressful situations it can start in adulthood. Genetic vulnerability has also been proven through twin studies (Kendler et al cited by Gelder et al, 2000). Phobia that persists from childhood has poor prognosis as compared to one starting in adulthood. Treatment is through graded exposure to the feared objects or situation. This can reduce the intensity of fear but rarely is phobia</p>
<p>cleared completely.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="social-phobia">Social Phobia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#social-phobia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This refers to inappropriate anxiety experienced in situations where one is observed and criticised. Such places include restaurants, canteens, dinner parties, seminars, board meetings, or other such situations where one may be observed and criticised.</p>
<p>It is wrong to avoid the feared objects or situations. Psychological treatment is recommended. This includes:</p>
<ul>
<li>Social skill training</li>
<li>Relaxation</li>
<li>Exposure to feared situation</li>
</ul>
<p>Cognitive behaviour therapy is the most appropriate treatment where exposure is combined with management of anxiety.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="agoraphobia">Agoraphobia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#agoraphobia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is fear experienced by anxious individuals while away from home, in crowds or in situations that they cannot leave easily. In addition to the general features in phobia, agoraphobia has frequent symptoms such depression, depersonalisation, and obsessional thoughts. Situations that provoke fear include buses, trains, shops, and supermarkets. Occasionally, agoraphobia may be precipitated by anticipatory anxiety.</p>
<p>Cognitive hypothesis says that agoraphobia develops in people who are unreasonably afraid of minor physical illness. The biological hypothesis says that the initial anxiety attack results from environmental stimuli acting on those already predisposed.</p>
<p>Meanwhile, the psychoanalytic hypothesis attributes agoraphobia to unacceptable sexual or aggressive impulses. It is commonly found in people who are dependent and who tend to avoid rather than confront the problem.</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>With regard to treatment, in the early stages, patients are counselled to return to the situation they are avoiding. Later stages require behaviour therapy combined with exposure to the feared situations. Anxiolytics are used on special occasions when the patient is expected to undertake important engagement. However, drugs are discouraged in order to avoid dependence. Antidepressants are used to treat accompanying depressive disorder.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="disorders-of-infancy-and-childhood">Disorders of Infancy and Childhood<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#disorders-of-infancy-and-childhood" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>It is important to remember that you should keep in mind that there are special features of working with children. Unlike adults, children are not free and independent. Children are always tied to or dependent upon their parents or some comparable caretakers. They are usually brought for treatment by their parents.</p>
<p>It is impossible, therefore, to make lasting progress without involving either their parents or guardians. The nurse is required to establish good rapport with both the child and the parents/parent substitute. If this is not done, the nurse can easily be left with the child by the frustrated parents.</p>
<p>It is worth noting that children come into treatment with limited coping skills, less developed defence mechanisms and less ability to conceptualise than adults. Nurses should, therefore, avoid direct interpretation of the child's behaviour so as to avoid overwhelming the child with anxiety.</p>
<p>Since children have less impulse control and awareness of control than adults, they need clear and firm limits. In order to assess the child's feelings and problems, it can be useful to use drawings, dolls, puppets, clay and games.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="autistic-disorders">Autistic Disorders<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#autistic-disorders" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Autism is a state of psychotic disorder which has its onset in early infancy. Initially the disorder was associated with an unsatisfactory mother-infant relationship. This belief is held to be true to this day, however, researchers continue to examine the potential genetic, biochemical, and physical bases for the disorder.</p>
<p>Autistic children are characterised by several symptoms, including:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Self-involvement</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Withdrawal</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Severe impairment in verbal communication and in interpersonal relationships</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Bizarre stereotyped or ritualistic responses to the environment</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="management-of-autistic-disorders">Management of Autistic Disorders<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management-of-autistic-disorders" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Nursing intervention includes observation to ensure safety and to assist in understanding the individual. It also involves establishment of a one-to-one relationship for support and coordination of services.</p>
<p>Phenothiazine medications are frequently used with psychotic children and adolescents. The nurse's role is to teach the family about the therapeutic effects and side effects of such substances.</p>
<p>Be aware that the parents of children diagnosed as autistic tend to blame themselves and may be overcome by guilt. They, therefore, require special support, teaching and reassurance. The nurse should focus on the mother's significance to her child, enhancing her self-esteem and then move to a discussion of specific plans and approaches to the condition.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="developmental-deviations-and-specific-behavioural-symptoms">Developmental Deviations and Specific Behavioural Symptoms<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#developmental-deviations-and-specific-behavioural-symptoms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The term developmental deviation implies that a child's maturation is significantly delayed in one or more areas. Developmental deviation may occur in almost any aspect of the child's physical, psychological or social status. However, it is most common in relation to motor, speech and cognitive areas.</p>
<p>Teachers most often detect developmental deviations in pre-school and early school. The most permanent and pathological symptoms in older children are enuresis and sometimes encopresis.</p>
<p>Other forms of antisocial behaviour may result when the child has experienced long periods of frustration or ridicule because of developmental deficits. Such behaviour includes lying (hoping to make them feel important or successful), stealing often focused on taking what the child feels they are not able to earn or achieve and setting fires as a display of mastery or power. Such behaviour and symptoms are problems in themselves, and they contribute to the child's sense of inadequacy and interpersonal isolation.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="nursing-intervention">Nursing Intervention<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nursing-intervention" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>With regard to developmental deviation, attention must be focused on biopsychosocial assessment. Emphasis is placed on early detection, parental education, and support for the child or adolescent. Family members should</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>be involved early enough so that they can understand the deviations and adjust their expectations. This can help to prevent social disruptions that can often result from developmental deviations. The nurse can assist parents in establishing clear, consistent and reasonable responses to inappropriate behaviour while underlying causes and solutions are being explored.</p>
<p>Limited behaviour modification plans, negotiated jointly with the parents and child, are often useful in these circumstances. Control of symptoms tends to relieve the overwhelming anxiety of parents and return a sense of security to the child or adolescent. It also limits the adverse consequences of the child's socially obnoxious or self-injurious behaviour, and frees some family energy for the process of examining the causes and long-term solutions.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="attention-deficit-hyperactivity-disorder-adhd">Attention Deficit Hyperactivity Disorder (ADHD)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#attention-deficit-hyperactivity-disorder-adhd" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This problem is referred to as hyperactivity and is characterised by impulsivity, excessive motor activity, and difficulties in sustaining attention. It is the most common cause of psychological referrals to mental health and paediatric facilities, that is, 16.1% of all types of ADHD (Woolrich, Hannah et al, cited by Carson et al, 2000).</p>
<p>ADHD occurs more in boys than in girls, with a ratio of 9:1. The greatest frequency of occurrence is before the age of eight. Numbers decline thereafter with briefer episodes. Residual difficulties like attention deficit may continue into adolescence or adulthood.</p>
<p>The child may be observed to have any of the following features:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Excessive or exaggerated muscular activity such as aimless or haphazard running or fidgeting.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Difficulty in sustaining attention.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Being highly distractible.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Failure to follow instructions or respond to demands made on them.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Having impulsive behaviour as well as low tolerance to frustration.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>They tend to be of low intelligence due to hyperactivity, with an IQ of 7-15 below the average.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>They are socially intrusive and immature.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>They have a high rate of driving offences when they attain adolescence.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>They do not get along with their parents since they do not obey them.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Their negative behaviours tend to make their peers to view them negatively.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>They usually do poorly in school with specific learning disabilities such as difficulties in reading or learning basic subjects in school.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>The causal factors in the Attention Deficit Hyperactivity Disorder are not clear but genetic and social environment are said to contribute significantly. Other factors cited are parental personality problem (Marrison, cited by Carson et al 2000). There is also some speculation relating to dietary factors, especially food colouring, but the latter theory has been partially discredited. Psychological causes have not been conclusive, that is, no psychological cause has been identified.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="treatments-and-outcomes">Treatments and Outcomes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatments-and-outcomes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Cerebral stimulants like amphetamines have a quieting effect on children (Pelham, et al cited by Carson 2000). They decrease over activity and distractibility. At the same time, they increase their attention and their ability to concentrate. This enables them to function better at school. Though it does not cure hyperactivity, it is able to reduce behavioural symptoms in about two thirds of the cases.</p>
<p>Certain drugs may be used. These include ritalin (methylphenidate), which is an amphetamine and pamoline, which has been found to be useful in adolescents and young adults. The latter is taken over a long period.</p>
<p>Short-term effects of stimulants are well known but long-term effects are yet to be established. Note that there are similarities between methylphenidate (ritalin) and cocaine, which causes concern since it can be abused.</p>
<p>The other form of treatment is behaviour therapy, featuring positive reinforcement of acceptable responses from the patient, for example, providing immediate praise. It is noted to be good in short-term gains.</p>
<p>When behaviour modification and medication are combined, good results are observed in ADHD. However, the latter appears to be more effective than the former (Pelham and Colleagues, cited by Carson et al 2000).</p>
<p>Drugs should be used cautiously only on those children who do not benefit from other alternative forms of treatment</p>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="adhd-beyond-adolescence">ADHD Beyond Adolescence<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#adhd-beyond-adolescence" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Even without treatment, hyperactive behaviour tends to decrease by the time the children attain their middle teens.</p>
<p>It has been noted that there was less education among young adults who had a history of hyperactivity in childhood. There were also more auto accidents among the hyperactive children. However, only a minority continued to display anti-social behaviour into adulthood or developed psychopathologies. Major depressive disorders were rare among ADHD patients (Alpert, Maddocks et al cited by Carson et al 2000) but a small percentage of the patient population did develop psychological problems like being aggressive, abusing drugs in their late teens and early adulthood.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="mental-retardation">Mental Retardation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mental-retardation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In DSM IV, (Diagnostic and statistical manual iv) mental retardation is defined as 'significantly sub-average general intellectual functioning, that is accompanied by significant limitations in adaptive functioning' in such areas like self-care, work and safety. For one to be diagnosed as having mental retardation, the problem should have started before the age of seventeen years. If it occurs thereafter, it is considered as dementia. Mental retardation occurs among children throughout the world. It tends to increase in severity with age starting at five to six and reaches the peak at aged 15. This increase reflects changes in the demands made on the child by the family and the community as a whole.</p>
<p>Mental retardation is often a burden to parents and a socio-economic burden to the community. Mildly retarded children are the majority and usually appear normal in early stages. With adequate resources for education, they are able to learn acceptable social and survival skills such that they lose the stigma of being identified as mentally retarded.</p>
<p>There are several levels of mental retardation:</p>
<ul>
<li>Borderline Mental Retardation: 71-84 IQ</li>
<li>Mild Mental Retardation: 50-55 to 70 IQ</li>
<li>Moderate Mental Retardation: 35-40 to 50-55 IQ</li>
<li>Severe Mental Retardation: 20-25 to 35-40 IQ</li>
<li>Profound Mental Retardation: below 20-25 IQ</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="borderline-mental-retardation-71-84-iq">Borderline Mental Retardation: 71-84 IQ<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#borderline-mental-retardation-71-84-iq" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>With early diagnosis, parental assistance and educational programs, they can adjust socially, master simple academic and occupational skills, and become self-reliant.</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Borderline Mental Retardation: 71-84 IQ</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Mild Mental Retardation: 50-55 to 70 IQ</td></tr><tr><td>Moderate Mental Retardation: 35-40 to 50-55 IQ</td></tr><tr><td>Severe Mental Retardation: 20-25 to 35-40 IQ</td></tr><tr><td>Profound Mental Retardation: below 20-25 IQ</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="mild-mental-retardation-50-55-to-70-iq">Mild Mental Retardation: 50-55 to 70 IQ<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mild-mental-retardation-50-55-to-70-iq" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>They form the majority of those diagnosed as mentally retarded. They do not show any brain pathology and for this reason are capable of schooling. However, they require supervision since they are unable to foresee the consequences of their actions.</p>
<p>Like borderline mental retardation, they can adjust socially, master simple academic and occupational skills, and become self-reliant.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="moderate-mental-retardation-35-40-to-50-55-iq">Moderate Mental Retardation: 35-40 to 50-55 IQ<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#moderate-mental-retardation-35-40-to-50-55-iq" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>They are considered trainable, that is, they can master some routine skills like cooking if provided with specialised training in such activities.</p>
<p>They are observed to have the following characteristics:</p>
<ul>
<li>Low learning ability and not able to conceptualise.</li>
<li>Physically they appear clumsy, with poor motor coordination and may suffer from bodily deformities.</li>
<li>Though they appear unthreatening, they may turn out to be hostile and aggressive.</li>
<li>They can acquire partial independence with early diagnosis, parental help and</li>
</ul>
<p>Bishop's Computers
Bishop's Computers</p>
<p>adequate opportunities for training.</p>
<ul>
<li>In rare occasions, moderately retarded people may be gifted with specialised skills like outstanding musical ability. This phenomenon is documented but not adequately explained.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="severe-mental-retardation-20-25-to-35-40-iq">Severe Mental Retardation: 20-25 to 35-40 IQ<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#severe-mental-retardation-20-25-to-35-40-iq" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>These individuals are sometimes referred to as 'dependent retarded'. They are observed to have severely retarded speech and motor development. Sensory and motor handicaps are also common among the severely mentally retarded.</p>
<p>They are able to develop limited levels of personal hygiene and self-care skills that make them less dependent on others and although they are dependent on others for care, they can perform simple occupational tasks under supervision after specialised training.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="profound-mental-retardation-below-20-25-iq">Profound Mental Retardation: below 20-25 IQ<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#profound-mental-retardation-below-20-25-iq" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This condition is sometimes referred to as 'life support retarded'. Patients suffering from this condition are usually deficient in adaptive behaviour and unable to master any but the simplest tasks. Physical deformities, central nervous system pathology and retarded growth are not uncommon features. Other problems include convulsive seizures, mutism, deafness, low resistance to disease, poor health: all this combined make life expectancy quite short.</p>
<p>The condition is easily diagnosed in infancy due to obvious physical deformities and grossly delayed development. They remain under custodial care throughout their lives.</p>
<p>The condition has a known organic pathology: it is similar to dementia except in the case of a history of prior normal functioning in case of dementia. It has its origin in genetic factors, often due to chromosomal influence and it runs in families, for example, Down's syndrome.</p>
<p>The condition may originate in infections and toxic agents, especially during foetal development. It is also often seen in cases of prematurity and physical trauma.
